    <a title="Heather" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7371672-heather"><img alt="Heather" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588912125p2/7371672.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/712563011?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather" href="/user/show/7371672-heather">Heather</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781441806468" href="/book/show/6669331-dauntless">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7371672-heather?shelf=sci-fi-contemporary">sci-fi-contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer712563011" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15567077623204473957">It is rare to run across military sci-fi that is quick to read and not very complex, but this book is both of those things. The author theorizes - what if there was a King Arthur type character in sci-fi? A "dead" hero is discovered in stasis 100 years later. The book explores how well he is received (adoration and distrust), how does he fit in, etc. The entire novel is several space battles. The author does an excellent job of doing play-by-play of space battles that doesn't sound bogged down w</span>
  <span id="freeText15567077623204473957" style="display:none">It is rare to run across military sci-fi that is quick to read and not very complex, but this book is both of those things. The author theorizes - what if there was a King Arthur type character in sci-fi? A "dead" hero is discovered in stasis 100 years later. The book explores how well he is received (adoration and distrust), how does he fit in, etc. The entire novel is several space battles. The author does an excellent job of doing play-by-play of space battles that doesn't sound bogged down with details or confusing. The only other content is mostly a heavy-handed "back in my day we did stuff right!" inner dialogue from the main character. The point is that the fleet has lost their war ethics and discipline and that these two are related. Also, they lost their ability to use military strategy.<br /><br />All in all, the book was only mildly interesting. The characters are unimaginative and not well developed. There is almost no world building. There is too slim of plot which leads to over emphasizing the the few main points. The politics parts to the novel consist of just a constant interrupting vice president who for some reason gets to hang out on the bridge. I'm sure the next novels must be more interesting, but this was was just okay. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15567077623204473957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1958558134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1958558134" />
    <a title="Ozymandias" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22223147-ozymandias"><img alt="Ozymandias" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373892974p2/22223147.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1958558134?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ozymandias" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ozymandias" href="/user/show/22223147-ozymandias">Ozymandias</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/23823501-dauntless">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22223147-ozymandias?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22223147-ozymandias?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1958558134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7797196625809727665">I enjoyed this book because of its inversion of the usual paradigm. Generally speaking, it's the guy from the future who comes to the past with enough knowledge and skill to make him seem superhuman. This time around it's a guy from the past who comes to the future (through an all-too-plausible cryostasis pod failure rather than time travel) and finds himself basically a superman. The reason for this is because the war, whose first battle he was lost in, has dragged on for a century. All the bes</span>
  <span id="freeText7797196625809727665" style="display:none">I enjoyed this book because of its inversion of the usual paradigm. Generally speaking, it's the guy from the future who comes to the past with enough knowledge and skill to make him seem superhuman. This time around it's a guy from the past who comes to the future (through an all-too-plausible cryostasis pod failure rather than time travel) and finds himself basically a superman. The reason for this is because the war, whose first battle he was lost in, has dragged on for a century. All the best captains were wiped out through attrition before they could train proper replacements and now the entire starfleet's main combat tactic is to charge heroically and never back down. And worse still, they attribute that style of fighting to the lead character, John Geary, who was conveniently and heroically dead and therefore suitable as the basis of all sorts of heroic legends.<br /><br />That's hook one. Hook two is the fact that they only recover him on their way to the climactic and glorious last battle of the war, which turns out naturally to have been a colossal trap. So now John Geary finds himself still adjusting to 100 years of change and in command of an entire fleet of battleships, none of whom are really used to discipline or obedience, trapped in the home system of the enemy with the enemy fleet between them and escape. Hoo boy. So now he has to lead his forces to escape against all odds, while rebuilding his fleet into the disciplined unit he knew and coming to terms with his new life.<br /><br />The novel is heavily influenced by Xenophon's <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20686810.Lost_in_Asia_Xenophon_s_Anabasis" title="Lost in Asia Xenophon's Anabasis by Xenophon" rel="nofollow noopener">Anabasis</a>
</i>, with its tale of an army trapped in enemy territory struggling just to get home. That's a good basis for a novel. The scientific aspects of the universe are very well thought out, with detection systems limited by the speed of light and not one but two different types of FTL travel, each with their own limitations. The book is only the first in a series, so don't go in expecting a real ending, but while the series does last for what seems like forever this is the truly standout one. Geary's story is completely relatable, for all that it's so bizarre, and the description of ship-to-ship combat in space is logical enough to make the action scenes exciting. Physical limitations drive the plot rather than impeding it. In all, it's a good entry in the military space opera subgenre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7797196625809727665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_538356377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/538356377" />
    <a title="Eric Allen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6229358-eric-allen"><img alt="Eric Allen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560117879p2/6229358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/538356377?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric Allen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric Allen" href="/user/show/6229358-eric-allen">Eric Allen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer538356377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14491202613549912010">Dauntless<br/>Book One of The Lost Fleet<br/>By Jack Campbell<br/><br/>A Review by Eric Allen<br/><br/>Jack Campbell is the pseudonym for one John G. Hemry, a retired US Navy ship pilot. His long experience in this profession lends quite a bit of realism to his writings of large scale space battles and the way crewmen aboard ships work. After retiring from his career in the Navy, he set about persuing his lifelong dream of becoming an author, and that leads us to his Lost Fleet series.<br/><br/>John "Black Jack" Geary has been awakene</span>
  <span id="freeText14491202613549912010" style="display:none">Dauntless<br />Book One of The Lost Fleet<br />By Jack Campbell<br /><br />A Review by Eric Allen<br /><br />Jack Campbell is the pseudonym for one John G. Hemry, a retired US Navy ship pilot. His long experience in this profession lends quite a bit of realism to his writings of large scale space battles and the way crewmen aboard ships work. After retiring from his career in the Navy, he set about persuing his lifelong dream of becoming an author, and that leads us to his Lost Fleet series.<br /><br />John "Black Jack" Geary has been awakened from cold sleep aboard an escape pod an entire century after the battle that nearly killed him to find that the war in which he made his last stand against the enemy Syndics is still raging, and he has come to a state of near worship for the battle in which he almost lost his life. Appalled by the hero worship he faces, and by the degredation of military tactics that the Navy has suffered in the century he was asleep, he reluctantly assumes command of the fleet after all of the command staff are executed after a stunning defeat above the Syndic homeworld.<br /><br />Using skills he learned in the Navy of a hundred years ago, Geary leads the fleet in retreat back toward Alliance space, dealing with military politics and drama, and trying to train the untamed rabble of the fleet into a respectable fighting unit.<br /><br />The Good? The space battles in this book are extraordinarily well thought out, and it is plain to see how Campbell's decades as a Navy ship pilot influences his work, giving it a realism that is rarely seen in works of fiction. He understands the physics and mechanics behind moving a ship, and behind movements and formations of an entire fleet. He knows his military tactics quite well, and all of the pros and cons involved in employing them. You can tell that he has real experience as an officer and knows all the little annoyances that come with being in charge. All of this is incorporated flawlessly into the story. Frankly, just about any Science Fiction writer in the world could learn a thing or two about writing believeable space battles from Campbell. He really shines at it.<br /><br />The setting is pretty interesting as well. The century old war with a long retreat back home ahead of a fleet full of rather incompetant officers that balk at following orders lends a very interesting and believable backdrop to the events of the story. These combine with Campbell's military knowledge to create a rather fun and realistc atmosphere.<br /><br />Geary goes through some rather good character development, starting as a rather shell shocked officer that lacks confidence in himself, to the leader that the fleet needs in order to hold everything together and get home. He goes through a lot of inner struggle over quite a few issues and really starts to come into his own as a commander by the end of the book.<br /><br />The Bad? Frankly, Campbell writes like a military commander. Where this is exemplary in detailing all the little draws on Geary's time, and in the space battles, it is not so much when it comes to the characters and the pacing. Geary is the only real character in this book. He's the only one with anything resembling a personality, or a character arc. Everyone else is portrayed the way a military man would portray them, as replaceable, expendable cogs in the great warmachine. Nameless, faceless soldiers that exist only to fulfil their function. None of them have real personalities or are allowed to shine as people instead of the part they play in the operations of the fleet. One or two give a slight glimmer here and there for the sake of Geary having someone to interact with, but the vast majority of them are nothing more than cardblard cutouts. <br /><br />There are several instances where Geary loses ships and goes through some patches of guilt and self-doubt, but these fall flat because we never get to see any of the people who die on these ships ourselves. We only experience them as numbers in a tally of the dead. To the reader, these are just numbers. They're not characters. They're not people who lost their lives in battle. They're only numbers. We can't connect emotionally with people we don't see and interact with in the story, so when they die by the thousands, we don't really feel anything for them.<br /><br />None of the characters in this book get phyiscal descriptions, not even Geary himself. None of the ships are described in any meaningful way other than their size, weapon's payload, and use to the fleet. None of the rooms or hallways that Geary visits are described in any meaningful way. The minute details that make a story feel real, things so simple as the texture of the carpet, the color of someone's eyes, a person's unique manerisms and speach patterns, or the dimensions of a room, all of these are completely absent from the story. <br /><br />The story is not very well paced. There's real tension throughout most of the book as we watch Geary grinding his teeth over the slowness of fleet movements, but it's static. It never increases or decreases. It doesn't build up to any sort of meaningful climax. There is a rather large battle at the end of the book. However, the tone and pacing of the story does not change or build into the battle so it doesn't feel like the huge, climactic event that it was meant to be.<br /><br />In conclusion, this book was rather entertaining. The space battles are spectacular, and Geary is a likeable and entertaining character. Where this book falls flat are the pacing, the secondary characters, and the descriptive elements. Campbell got all of the tactics and physics right, but he left out the most important element. The HUMAN element. This is the element that really draws a person into a story, and it was almost completely absent. Three stars is the best I can give. It was entertaining, and I do want to read more, but it could have been so much better than it was. I'll give the series another book or two before I make a decision on whether it's worth the time to read or not.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/6229358?shelf=read" rel="nofollow noopener"> Check out my other reviews.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14491202613549912010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2469459477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2469459477" />
    <a title="C.T. Phipps" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5873287-c-t-phipps"><img alt="C.T. Phipps" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401836765p2/5873287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2469459477?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C.T. Phipps" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C.T. Phipps" href="/user/show/5873287-c-t-phipps">C.T. Phipps</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5873287-c-t-phipps?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5873287-c-t-phipps?shelf=lost-fleet">lost-fleet</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2469459477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14862795408278095926"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://booknest.eu/reviews/charles/1720-dauntless'>http://booknest.eu/reviews/charles/17...</a><br/><br/>4/5<br/><br/>DAUNTLESS is the first novel in the Lost Fleet series which has the premise of Captain John "Black Jack" Geary being a officer who has been stuck in stasis for a century. Much to his dismay, he finds out his heroic last stand has been elevated to Davey Crocket at the Alamo/King Arthur levels of proportion. So much so that he is treated as the Second Coming when he is finally rescued, as well as the man who is to deliver them from the same enemy he "died</span>
  <span id="freeText14862795408278095926" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/charles/1720-dauntless">http://booknest.eu/reviews/charles/17...</a><br /><br />4/5<br /><br />DAUNTLESS is the first novel in the Lost Fleet series which has the premise of Captain John "Black Jack" Geary being a officer who has been stuck in stasis for a century. Much to his dismay, he finds out his heroic last stand has been elevated to Davey Crocket at the Alamo/King Arthur levels of proportion. So much so that he is treated as the Second Coming when he is finally rescued, as well as the man who is to deliver them from the same enemy he "died" fighting so many years ago.<br /><br />It's not a bad premise, though I think Jack Campbell overdoes it. Tactics have degraded in the future to the point everyone just flies at the enemy and gets killed, he's constantly reminded of how awesome he is (while thinking he's not), and everyone who is skeptical of him is either evil or dramatically overreacting. One character assumes he's going to either get them all killed in a heroic attack or try to take over the Alliance. Despite this, I very much enjoyed the setting and liked the focus on the laws and customs of war. John is a person who comes from a more civilized time and is appalled by the treatment of prisoners in the present. I think few books bother to treat the "enemy" characters as anything but targets so this was a nice change of pace.<br /><br />The starship battles are well-designed with a focus on fleet action as well as real-world tactics (applied to space) like formation, training, and the importance of discipline. Jack Campbell worked in the Navy and its influence on his work is obvious. It's nice to see someone who bucks the usual trend of military mavericks. Jack just wants a disciplined well-oiled machine and he's got a bunch of Leeroy Jenkins in starships.<br /><br />Characterization-wise, most of the people involved are a bit broad. John Geary is the "only sane man" on the ship as everyone is just determined to kill the enemy by attacking relentlessly. He's also a subject of great suspicion by the civilian governments because they have no experience with any officers who don't have utter contempt for them. There's some similarities to Captain America as well as King Arthur with a moral paragon waking up to take the reigns, even as John is nothing like the tough-talking badass that history has made him out to be.<br /><br />I feel like there's some missed opportunities here as Jack never really takes time to mourn anyone who might have died while he was asleep as well as the sheer culture shock of waking up a century later. Instead, he's all business and his problems are primarily with how the Navy has degenerated into a band of pirates. I feel like giving him a Peggy Carter equivalent or have him react to his initial awakening would have been more interesting.<br /><br />On the other hand, I have to say I'm very impressed with the villains. I very much enjoyed the idea of the enemy being an ultra-capitalist cyberpunk state run by corporations. That's something you don't see often in military science-fiction and while they mostly remain targets, they eventually do get fleshed out in future books (particularly the spin-offs).<br /><br />Speaking as an author of space opera fiction, I'm always on the lookout for new or classic fiction in the genre to read. Mind you, I'm one of those lumps who originally had his exposure limited to Star Trek and Star Wars. I've always thought of it as a visual medium so while I love Babylon Five, Battlestar Galactica, Mass Effect, and Halo--I never quite got into the literary side of things. Well, you know, until I decided to write it myself. After writing Lucifer's Star, I did become an afficianado of military science fiction and have read many authors I've since come to love. Lost Fleet is an excellent example of the genre and one that was recommended to me many times before I picked it up.<br /><br />In conclusion, this is an entertaining fun book even if it's not something that I absolutely felt I had to read. Still, if you're looking for deep characterization and a less-than-perfect hero then this isn't the series for you. On my end? I don't mind a little competence porn and read the entire series. Those who love big epic space battles and a guy trying to bring an unruly crew together will find a lot to love in these books. Dauntless is only the start of an epic series and it continues right until a truly epic climax (no spoilers).</span>
  <a data-text-id="14862795408278095926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1159429720" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1159429720" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1159429720?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/7066461-dauntless">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Returned heroes, RTS space combat gamers, fans of Battlestar Galactica</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=military-fiction">military-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1159429720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13545010715088808044">I have been hearing about this series forever. I'm generally somewhat indifferent to military SF — spaceship battles alone are not particularly compelling to me, unless I'm controlling the ships in a game. Much of <i>Dauntless</i> seems inspired by spaceship combat games, where you get to build a fleet, choose your weapons, select leaders based on their attributes, and so on.<br/><br/>The Alliance has been at war with the Syndics for a century now. Captain John Geary was in command during an early battle in that</span>
  <span id="freeText13545010715088808044" style="display:none">I have been hearing about this series forever. I'm generally somewhat indifferent to military SF — spaceship battles alone are not particularly compelling to me, unless I'm controlling the ships in a game. Much of <i>Dauntless</i> seems inspired by spaceship combat games, where you get to build a fleet, choose your weapons, select leaders based on their attributes, and so on.<br /><br />The Alliance has been at war with the Syndics for a century now. Captain John Geary was in command during an early battle in that war. Then he went into emergency hibernation and was presumed dead; meanwhile, legends of his leadership have grown all out of proportion, so when the Alliance fleet unexpectedly finds his survival pod and revives him, much of the fleet reacts as if King Arthur has returned to save Britain.<br /><br />Through a very briefly described sequence of events, Captain Geary is left in charge of the fleet while its leaders go to surrender to the Syndic forces who have just defeated them after a disastrous attack in the middle of Syndic space. Since the Syndics are cartoon sci-fi villains, they execute all the Alliance brass and then demand unconditional surrender from the fleet. Geary figures out a strategy to get away, and leads the fleet in a retreat through a series of star systems, trying to pick up supplies and fight off pursuing Syndics all the way.<br /><br />Unfortunately, Captain Geary was never the epic hero his legend has become, and he soon finds that the Alliance has changed in the hundred years he was in suspended animation. Now they're more like rapacious glory hounds than a disciplined military, taking Geary's old command advice literally and out of context to mean that they should never retreat. They've also stopped following the rules of war that were observed in Geary's time, so besides convincing his new subordinates to follow orders and not just charge into battle to score as many victory points as possible, he also has to convince them that they shouldn't simply throw POWs out airlocks, or bombard civilian planets.<br /><br />That's about the extent of the characterization and the plot. Captain Geary is idolized and hero-worshipped, but he also has a bunch of ship captains who think he's a relic from the wrong time. He stands for <s>American</s>Alliance values like honor, discipline, professionalism, and civilized treatment of the enemy while his descendants have become more like barbarian warriors. There are several battles and some examples of Geary trying to show the fleet the "right" way to do things, but the book ends with the Alliance forces still being chased, deep in Syndic territory, so obviously the series will go on as long as the author can keep spinning it out.<br /><br />As military SF goes, <i>Dauntless</i> is pretty much the arch-typical example. It's not new or brilliant, it's just a spaceship combat story written for people who like spaceship combat. I liked the story well enough and the writing served its purpose, but I'm really not drawn into the saga enough to want to continue what is already a fairly long series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13545010715088808044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_225732114" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225732114" />
    <a title="An Odd1" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863716-an-odd1"><img alt="An Odd1" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390427996p2/4863716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225732114?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="An Odd1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="An Odd1" href="/user/show/4863716-an-odd1">An Odd1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225732114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer99317621101180506">"The Lost Fleet: Dauntless" (Lost Fleet #1) by Jack Campbell is the flagship of the Alliance fleet that rescues the almost dead stasis pod of John "Black Jack" Geary, posthumous Captain, one hundred years after his final battle, in charge of three ships. That first enemy surprise attack has not yet been explained. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[Evidence now points to another secret alien enemy, material for sequels. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> His last order then, suited for the particular circumstance in time, has become </span>
  <span id="freeText99317621101180506" style="display:none">"The Lost Fleet: Dauntless" (Lost Fleet #1) by Jack Campbell is the flagship of the Alliance fleet that rescues the almost dead stasis pod of John "Black Jack" Geary, posthumous Captain, one hundred years after his final battle, in charge of three ships. That first enemy surprise attack has not yet been explained. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Evidence now points to another secret alien enemy, material for sequels. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> His last order then, suited for the particular circumstance in time, has become military mantra applied to all situations willy-nilly, individual aggression the culture, his name a byword, an oath, a legend. Only John remembers how to co-ordinate complicated precise formation manouevers. Ambushed instead of a surprise attack on the Syndic homeworld, the fleet faces annihilation after the enemy massacres the peace negotiating team of senior officers. Can John teach co-operation, to follow orders as a team? <br /><br />A small unguarded gate can get them to Corvus. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The successful escape costs his own great-nephew and ship. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> From there, more choices, time to refuel and repair. Syndics never keep their word - could merchant ships not have been at least partly unloaded<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ before destruction <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>? If Geary takes a chance, fights with barely trained armada, their angry needs may be satisfied, but at what cost? He has tough choices, and feedback seems limited to subservient or resistant, spirits of the ancestors his only neutral audience. I feel more sorry for him than admiration. <br /><br />Co-President Victoria Rione constantly criticizes, and harshly warns she will prevent him from returning triumphant and victorious to rule Earth. We know he does not want what he has already - jumping the gun much? Repeated questioning of his motives and honor is annoying. "If I tripped and fell on my butt they'd probably think it was Black Jack Geary's way of preparing for action, and they'd all start doing it" p159. He acts with honor toward prisoners, when before they were killed, so how can anyone doubt his virtue? <br /><br />I wished the incompetent ship captains were booted out before they could cause damage, but understand caution of novice in big new system. "Vebos won't still be commanding the <i>Arrogant</i> when we leave this system" p143 happens sooner than I understood, but after mistake. Numos sacrifices Hatherian. <br /><br />Geary wins battles by thinking ahead, planning, projecting, guessing correctly, and inspiring followers. Advice feels like true military education. "The three things you need to worry about most during the hours leading up to an engagement are acting too early, acting too early, and acting too early" p243. The tedious arithmetic of seconds, minutes, delay between action and "time-late and distorted" instrument display gets monotonous. "At time zero seven, all units accelerate to point zero five light speed and proceed along formation axis defined by <i>Dauntless</i>" p251. I will probably read more to solve third alien species mystery, but have to skip quasi-science.<br /><br />Typos:<br />p198 "needed fixed" can be "needed fixing" or "needed to be fixed"</span>
  <a data-text-id="99317621101180506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_937135577" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/937135577" />
    <a title="Nico" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5244445-nico"><img alt="Nico" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1303137876p2/5244445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/937135577?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nico" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nico" href="/user/show/5244445-nico">Nico</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer937135577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14166308727222039693"><a href='http://gosureviews.blogspot.de/2014/07/die-verschollene-flotte-lost-fleet-1-6.html<br />' rel='nofollow noopener'>German Review on GosuReviews</a><br/><br/>I decided to do a review of the whole series(books 1-6) here, because all the books are relatively short.<br/><br/>These were the first books I read of John G. Hemry under his pseudonym Jack Campbell. He writes very gripping hard SciFi and I loved the enormous space battle scenarios throughout the book. Especially all the little nuances that get often times inadequately handled in other SciFi books. For example the time delay that orders take to travel within a fleet that is st</span>
  <span id="freeText14166308727222039693" style="display:none"><a href="http://gosureviews.blogspot.de/2014/07/die-verschollene-flotte-lost-fleet-1-6.html&lt;br /&gt;" rel="nofollow noopener">German Review on GosuReviews</a><br /><br />I decided to do a review of the whole series(books 1-6) here, because all the books are relatively short.<br /><br />These were the first books I read of John G. Hemry under his pseudonym Jack Campbell. He writes very gripping hard SciFi and I loved the enormous space battle scenarios throughout the book. Especially all the little nuances that get often times inadequately handled in other SciFi books. For example the time delay that orders take to travel within a fleet that is stretched over light minutes and a whole new approach to space battles, where these delays have an enormous influence. After having read this series I think you will look more skeptically on space battles in other scifi books. All this is very well explained and gives the series a unique feeling.<br />The Story is very clever done, with a believable plot, a troubling history and an amazing side plot that slowly( maybe too slowly) develops in the background. <br />I also enjoyed the three main characters, who get a fair bit of development, but get sadly stuck halfway through the series and their interactions in the later parts get very repetitive. And boy can this author write antagonists, very despicable opponents throughout.<br />These repetitions are the main weakness of the series in my opinion. Everything is very interesting in the beginning is very entertaining because it's new, but if you get a description of how Geary feels about the conference software in every book it kind of sucks. There are quite a few of these explaining repetitions throughout the books, that stand out if you read the six books back to back. The fault for this lies for me in the fact, that these books are so damn short and should have been written as a trilogy and edited accordingly.<br />This leads to a little lull in the action in book three and four. What happens in the last too books is then up to par again.<br /><br />Okay that got a little bit longer than I expected so here a <br /><b>tl;dr:<br />Great hard SciFi with amazingly innovative space battles and a gripping story. </b></span>
  <a data-text-id="14166308727222039693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_269916134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269916134" />
    <a title="Melissa McShane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane"><img alt="Melissa McShane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216147749p2/1329059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269916134?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa McShane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa McShane" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane">Melissa McShane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Battlestar Galactica and the Temeraire series</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=military-fiction">military-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=space-travel">space-travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269916134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14946450279321539381">I like military fiction anyway, and this is some good military SF. You've got this guy, John Geary, who wakes up from 100 years of drifting in an abandoned survival pod to find that a) the war that had just begun back then is STILL going on, b) he was "posthumously" promoted to captain after his disappearance, c) in all that time, he's become something of a folk hero, and d) thanks to fleet rules about seniority, when the Alliance fleet's leadership is massacred, he's the senior ranking officer </span>
  <span id="freeText14946450279321539381" style="display:none">I like military fiction anyway, and this is some good military SF. You've got this guy, John Geary, who wakes up from 100 years of drifting in an abandoned survival pod to find that a) the war that had just begun back then is STILL going on, b) he was "posthumously" promoted to captain after his disappearance, c) in all that time, he's become something of a folk hero, and d) thanks to fleet rules about seniority, when the Alliance fleet's leadership is massacred, he's the senior ranking officer by about 100 years, which means that e) he's suddenly become the leader of a fleet whose traditions bear no resemblance to the military discipline he's used to and f) has to fight not only the opposing forces of the Syndic, but also the captains and commanders who resist his every command. (Good thing there are so many letters in the alphabet, huh?)<br /><br />This first book in a six-volume series does a good job of establishing both the larger picture of two sides fighting an endless war and the small details of how the fleet works. As frustrating as Geary's internal opponents are (because most of them are total raving lunatics who've somehow internalized the idea that a glorious battle is one in which it doesn't matter if you die), the history of how they got to be that way makes a lot of sense. Campbell has an excellent grasp of physics and how it would affect ships traveling at relativistic speeds; no close-in Star Wars/Star Trek combat (by which I mean no criticism of either; this series could never make the kind of dramatic television and film they do). There are a few discordant notes, but certainly not enough to ruin my enjoyment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14946450279321539381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

		<td>
		    <b>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cO77nIC5gaw4XrZNb9mHJstHQSWCHxcL1ktCN1XCxb3uQlLZMJyWxT57AB50Z5LcdhxaIs0H4jMQuifBkRUluw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bht/3+bzzL82hEqmZYnyCgEjLapi8KInU+tptOc5OhHwt9aaVtbb1jCh/PV+N+fwvHg2rS3oVx+VGgxCI+7aFw==')})
  }
//]]>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5929608720808360699">Jack Campbell is a pseudonym for American science fiction author <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6461132.John_G_Hemry' rel='nofollow noopener'>John G. Hemry</a>.<br/><br/><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information.</a></i> <br/><br/>John G. Hemry is an American author of military science fiction novels. Drawing on his experience as a retired United States Navy officer, he has written the Stark's War and Paul Sinclair series.</span>
  <span id="freeText5929608720808360699" style="display:none">Jack Campbell is a pseudonym for American science fiction author <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6461132.John_G_Hemry" rel="nofollow noopener">John G. Hemry</a>.<br /><br /><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/44217-master-list-of-author-disambiguation-by-spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information.</a></i> <br /><br />John G. Hemry is an American author of military science fiction novels. Drawing on his experience as a retired United States Navy officer, he has written the Stark's War and Paul Sinclair series. Under the name Jack Campbell, he has written four volumes of the Lost Fleet series, and on his website names two more forthcoming volumes. He has also written over a dozen short stories, many published in Analog magazine, and a number of non-fiction works.<br /><br />John G Hemry is a retired United States Navy officer. His father, Jack M. Hemry, also served in the navy and as John points out was a mustang. John grew up living in several places including Pensacola, San Diego, and Midway Island.<br /><br />John graduated from Lyons High School in Lyons in 1974 then attended the US Naval Academy (Class of '78) where he was labeled 'the un-midshipman' by his roommates.<br /><br />He lives in Maryland with his wife and three kids. His two eldest children are diagnosed as autistic and suffer from Neuro immune dysfunction syndrome (NIDS), an auto-immune ailment which causes their illness, but are progressing under treatment.<br /><br />John is a member of the SFWA Musketeers whose motto reads: 'The Pen is Mightier Than the Sword, but the Wise Person Carries Both'.</span>
